<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Parthenon_from_west.jpg/16px-Parthenon_from_west.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Draig.svg/16px-Draig.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Draig.svg/24px-Draig.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Draig.svg/32px-Draig.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Myths" title="Portal:Myths">Myths portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Greek_mythology_sidebar" title="Template:Greek mythology sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Greek_mythology_sidebar" title="Template talk:Greek mythology sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Greek_mythology_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A major branch of <a href="/wiki/Classical_mythology" title="Classical mythology">classical mythology</a>, <b>Greek mythology</b> is the body of <a href="/wiki/Myth" title="Myth">myths</a> originally told by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_folklore" title="Ancient Greek folklore">Ancient Greek folklore</a>. These stories concern the <a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">origin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmology#Metaphysical_cosmology" title="Cosmology">nature of the world</a>, the lives and activities of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Greek_mythological_figures" title="List of Greek mythological figures">deities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_hero_cult" title="Greek hero cult">heroes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Greek_mythological_creatures" title="List of Greek mythological creatures">mythological creatures</a>, and the origins and significance of the ancient Greeks' own <a href="/wiki/Cult_(religious_practice)" title="Cult (religious practice)">cult</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">ritual</a> practices. Modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholars" title="Scholars">scholars</a> study the myths to shed light on the religious and political institutions of ancient Greece, and to better understand the nature of myth-making itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helios_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Helios-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greek myths were initially propagated in an <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral-poetic tradition</a> most likely by <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean</a> singers starting in the 18th century BC;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> eventually the myths of the heroes of the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a> and its aftermath became part of the oral tradition of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poems</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>.  Two poems by Homer's near contemporary <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Works_and_Days" title="Works and Days">Works and Days</a></i>, contain accounts of the genesis of the world, the succession of divine rulers, the succession of human ages, the origin of human woes, and the origin of sacrificial practices. Myths are also preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymns" title="Homeric Hymns">Homeric Hymns</a>, in fragments of <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poems</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Epic_Cycle" title="Epic Cycle">Epic Cycle</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">lyric poems</a>, in the works of the <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragedians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_comedy" title="Ancient Greek comedy">comedians</a> of the fifth century BC, in writings of scholars and poets of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic Age</a>, and in texts from the time of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> by writers such as <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>.
</p><p>Aside from this narrative deposit in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">ancient Greek literature</a>, pictorial representations of gods, heroes, and mythic episodes featured prominently in ancient <a href="/wiki/Pottery_of_ancient_Greece" title="Pottery of ancient Greece">vase paintings</a> and the decoration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Votive_deposit" title="Votive deposit">votive gifts</a> and many other artifacts. Geometric designs on pottery of the eighth century BC depict scenes from the Trojan cycle as well as the adventures of <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>. In the succeeding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_period_in_Greece" title="Archaic period in Greece">Archaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Greece" title="Hellenistic Greece">Hellenistic</a> periods, Homeric and various other mythological scenes appear, supplementing the existing literary evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Greek mythology has had an extensive influence on the culture, arts, and literature of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western civilization</a> and remains part of Western heritage and language. Poets and artists from ancient times to the present have derived inspiration from Greek mythology and have discovered contemporary significance and relevance in the themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 43">: 43 </span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achilles_Penthesileia_BM_B209.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="2275" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Achilles_Penthesileia_BM_B209.jpg/220px-Achilles_Penthesileia_BM_B209.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Achilles_Penthesileia_BM_B209.jpg/330px-Achilles_Penthesileia_BM_B209.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Achilles_Penthesileia_BM_B209.jpg/440px-Achilles_Penthesileia_BM_B209.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achilles_Penthesileia_BM_B209.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penthesileia" title="Penthesileia">Penthesileia</a> by Exekias, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 540 BC</span>, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>, London</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span></h2>
<p>Greek mythology is known today primarily from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">Greek literature</a> and representations on visual media dating from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geometric_Style" title="Geometric Style">Geometric period</a> from <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/circa#English" title="wikt:circa">c.</a><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 900 BC</span> to <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 800 BC</span> onward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf200_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graf200-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 200">: 200 </span></sup> In fact, literary and archaeological sources integrate, sometimes mutually supportive and sometimes in conflict; however, in many cases, the existence of this corpus of data is a strong indication that many elements of Greek mythology have strong factual and historical roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_sources">Literary sources</span></h3>
<p>Mythical narration plays an important role in nearly every genre of Greek literature. Nevertheless, the only general mythographical handbook to survive from Greek antiquity was the <i><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Pseudo-Apollodorus)" title="Bibliotheca (Pseudo-Apollodorus)">Library</a></i> of Pseudo-Apollodorus. This work attempts to reconcile the contradictory tales of the poets and provides a grand summary of traditional Greek mythology and heroic legends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hard1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hard1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Apollodorus_of_Athens" title="Apollodorus of Athens">Apollodorus of Athens</a> lived from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 180 BC</span> to <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 125 BC</span> and wrote on many of these topics. His writings may have formed the basis for the collection; however, the "Library" discusses events that occurred long after his death, hence the name Pseudo-Apollodorus.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prometheus_by_Gustave_Moreau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3101" data-file-width="1843" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Prometheus_by_Gustave_Moreau.jpg/170px-Prometheus_by_Gustave_Moreau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Prometheus_by_Gustave_Moreau.jpg/255px-Prometheus_by_Gustave_Moreau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Prometheus_by_Gustave_Moreau.jpg/340px-Prometheus_by_Gustave_Moreau.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prometheus_by_Gustave_Moreau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Prometheus" title="Prometheus">Prometheus</a></i> (1868 by <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Moreau" title="Gustave Moreau">Gustave Moreau</a>). The myth of Prometheus first was attested by <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> and then constituted the basis for a tragic trilogy of plays, possibly by Aeschylus, consisting of <i><a href="/wiki/Prometheus_Bound" title="Prometheus Bound">Prometheus Bound</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Prometheus_Unbound_(Aeschylus)" title="Prometheus Unbound (Aeschylus)">Prometheus Unbound</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prometheus_Pyrphoros" title="Prometheus Pyrphoros">Prometheus Pyrphoros</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Among the earliest literary sources are <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>'s two epic poems, the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>. Other poets completed the <a href="/wiki/Epic_Cycle" title="Epic Cycle">Epic Cycle</a>, but these later and lesser poems now are lost almost entirely. Despite their traditional name, the <i><a href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymns" title="Homeric Hymns">Homeric Hymns</a></i> have no direct connection with Homer. The oldest are choral hymns from the earlier part of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">Lyric age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles7_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miles7-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">: 7 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>, a possible contemporary with Homer, offers in his <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a> </i>(<i>Origin of the Gods</i>) the fullest account of the earliest Greek myths, dealing with the creation of the world, the origin of the gods, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_(mythology)" title="Giant (mythology)">Giants</a>, as well as elaborate genealogies, folktales, and aetiological myths. Hesiod's <i><a href="/wiki/Works_and_Days" title="Works and Days">Works and Days</a></i>, a didactic poem about farming life, also includes the myths of <a href="/wiki/Prometheus" title="Prometheus">Prometheus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pandora" title="Pandora">Pandora</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ages_of_Man#Hesiod's_Five_Ages" title="Ages of Man">Five Ages</a>. The poet advises on the best way to succeed in a dangerous world, rendered yet more dangerous by its gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lyrical poets often took their subjects from myth, but their treatment became gradually less narrative and more allusive. Greek lyric poets, including <a href="/wiki/Pindar" title="Pindar">Pindar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacchylides" title="Bacchylides">Bacchylides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simonides_of_Ceos" title="Simonides of Ceos">Simonides</a>, and bucolic poets such as <a href="/wiki/Theocritus" title="Theocritus">Theocritus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bion_of_Smyrna" title="Bion of Smyrna">Bion</a>, relate individual mythological incidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klatt-Brazouskixii_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klatt-Brazouskixii-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: xii">: xii </span></sup> Additionally, myth was central to classical <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_ancient_Greece" title="Theatre of ancient Greece">Athenian drama</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragic</a> playwrights <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a> took most of their plots from myths of the age of heroes and the Trojan War. Many of the great tragic stories (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Agamemnon" title="Agamemnon">Agamemnon</a> and his children, <a href="/wiki/Oedipus" title="Oedipus">Oedipus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jason" title="Jason">Jason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medea" title="Medea">Medea</a>, etc.) took on their classic form in these tragedies. The comic playwright <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">Aristophanes</a> also used myths, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Birds_(play)" title="The Birds (play)">The Birds</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Frogs" title="The Frogs">The Frogs</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles7_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miles7-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8">: 8 </span></sup>
</p><p>Historians <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>, and geographers <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, who traveled throughout the Greek world and noted the stories they heard, supplied numerous local myths and legends, often giving little-known alternative versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klatt-Brazouskixii_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Klatt-Brazouskixii-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: xii">: xii </span></sup> Herodotus in particular, searched the various traditions presented him and found the historical or mythological roots in the confrontation between Greece and the East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 60">: 60 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22">: 22 </span></sup> Herodotus attempted to reconcile origins and the blending of differing cultural concepts.
</p><p>The poetry of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> ages was primarily composed as a literary rather than cultic exercise. Nevertheless, it contains many important details that would otherwise be lost. This category includes the works of:
</p>
<ol><li>The Roman poets <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statius" title="Statius">Statius</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaius_Valerius_Flaccus" title="Gaius Valerius Flaccus">Valerius Flaccus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maurus_Servius_Honoratus" title="Maurus Servius Honoratus">Servius</a>'s commentary.</li>
<li>The Greek poets of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antique</a> period: <a href="/wiki/Nonnus" title="Nonnus">Nonnus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Liberalis" title="Antoninus Liberalis">Antoninus Liberalis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Smyrnaeus" title="Quintus Smyrnaeus">Quintus Smyrnaeus</a>.</li>
<li>The Greek poets of the Hellenistic period: <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius of Rhodes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">Callimachus</a>, Pseudo-<a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Parthenius_of_Nicaea" title="Parthenius of Nicaea">Parthenius</a>.</li></ol>
<p>Prose writers from the same periods who make reference to myths include <a href="/wiki/Apuleius" title="Apuleius">Apuleius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petronius" title="Petronius">Petronius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lollianus" title="Lollianus">Lollianus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Heliodorus_of_Emesa" title="Heliodorus of Emesa">Heliodorus</a>. Two other important non-poetical sources are the <i>Fabulae</i> and <i>Astronomica</i> of the Roman writer styled as Pseudo-<a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Hyginus" title="Gaius Julius Hyginus">Hyginus</a>, the <i>Imagines</i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philostratus_the_Elder" title="Philostratus the Elder">Philostratus the Elder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philostratus_the_Younger" title="Philostratus the Younger">Philostratus the Younger</a>, and the <i>Descriptions</i> of <a href="/wiki/Callistratus_(sophist)" title="Callistratus (sophist)">Callistratus</a>.
</p><p>Finally, several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> Greek writers provide important details of myth, much derived from earlier now lost Greek works. These preservers of myth include <a href="/wiki/Arnobius" title="Arnobius">Arnobius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hesychius_of_Alexandria" title="Hesychius of Alexandria">Hesychius</a>, the author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/John_Tzetzes" title="John Tzetzes">John Tzetzes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eustathius_of_Thessalonica" title="Eustathius of Thessalonica">Eustathius</a>. They often treat mythology from a Christian moralizing perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological_sources">Archaeological sources</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1136" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg/220px-RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg/330px-RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg/440px-RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomanVirgilFolio014rVergilPortrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, here depicted in the fifth-century manuscript, the <i><a href="/wiki/Vergilius_Romanus" title="Vergilius Romanus">Vergilius Romanus</a></i>, preserved details of Greek mythology in many of his writings.</div></div></div>
<p>The discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean civilization</a> by the German amateur <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Schliemann" title="Heinrich Schliemann">Heinrich Schliemann</a> in the nineteenth century, and the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> by the British archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Evans" title="Arthur Evans">Arthur Evans</a> in the twentieth century, helped to explain many existing questions about Homer's epics and provided archaeological evidence for many of the mythological details about gods and heroes. Unfortunately, the evidence about myths and rituals at Mycenaean and Minoan sites is entirely monumental, as the <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> script (an ancient form of Greek found in both Crete and mainland Greece) was used mainly to record inventories, although certain names of gods and heroes have been tentatively identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Geometric designs on pottery of the eighth-century  BC depict scenes from the Trojan cycle, as well as the adventures of Heracles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> These visual representations of myths are important for two reasons. Firstly, many Greek myths are attested on vases earlier than in literary sources: of the twelve labors of Heracles, for example, only the <a href="/wiki/Cerberus" title="Cerberus">Cerberus</a> adventure occurs in a contemporary literary text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HomerIliad366-369_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-HomerIliad366-369-14">[14]</a></sup> Secondly, visual sources sometimes represent myths or mythical scenes that are not attested in any extant literary source. In some cases, the first known representation of a myth in geometric art predates its first known representation in late archaic poetry, by several centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graf200_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Graf200-5">[5]</a></sup> In the Archaic (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 750</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 500 BC</span>), Classical (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 480</span>–323 BC), and Hellenistic (323–146 BC) periods, Homeric and various other mythological scenes appear, supplementing the existing literary evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Br-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Survey_of_mythic_history">Survey of mythic history</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phaedra-Color.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2623" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Phaedra-Color.jpg/220px-Phaedra-Color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Phaedra-Color.jpg/330px-Phaedra-Color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Phaedra-Color.jpg/440px-Phaedra-Color.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phaedra-Color.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phaedra_(mythology)" title="Phaedra (mythology)">Phaedra</a> with an attendant, probably her nurse, a fresco from <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 60</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 20 BC</span></div></div></div>
<p>Greek mythology has changed over time to accommodate the evolution of their culture, of which mythology, both overtly and in its unspoken assumptions, is an index of the changes. In Greek mythology's surviving literary forms, as found mostly at the end of the progressive changes, it is inherently political, as Gilbert Cuthbertson (1975) has argued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[i]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earlier inhabitants of the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkan Peninsula</a> were an agricultural people who, using <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a>, assigned a spirit to every aspect of nature. Eventually, these vague spirits assumed human forms and entered the local mythology as gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson17_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson17-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 17">: 17 </span></sup> When tribes from the north of the Balkan Peninsula invaded, they brought with them a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> of gods, based on conquest, force, prowess in battle, and violent heroism. Other older gods of the agricultural world fused with those of the more powerful invaders or else faded into insignificance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson17_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson17-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">: 18 </span></sup>
</p><p>After the middle of the Archaic period, myths about relationships between male gods and male heroes became more and more frequent, indicating the parallel development of <a href="/wiki/Pederasty_in_ancient_Greece" title="Pederasty in ancient Greece">pedagogic pederasty</a> (<span title="Ancient Greek-language text"><span lang="grc">παιδικὸς ἔρως</span></span>, <span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">eros paidikos</i></span>), thought to have been introduced around 630 BC. By the end of the fifth-century  BC, poets had assigned at least one <a href="/wiki/Eromenos" title="Eromenos">eromenos</a>, an adolescent boy who was their sexual companion, to every important <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">god</a> except <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a> and many legendary figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallimach109_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallimach109-18">[17]</a></sup> Previously existing myths, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patroclus" title="Patroclus">Patroclus</a>, also then were cast in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_myths_associated_with_same-sex_love" title="List of myths associated with same-sex love">pederastic light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Percy_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Percy-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 54">: 54 </span></sup> Alexandrian poets at first, then more generally literary mythographers in the early Roman Empire, often re-adapted stories of Greek mythological characters in this fashion.
</p><p>The achievement of epic poetry was to create story-cycles and, as a result, to develop a new sense of mythological chronology. Thus Greek mythology unfolds as a phase in the development of the world and of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowden11_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dowden11-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 11">: 11 </span></sup> While self-contradictions in these stories make an absolute timeline impossible, an approximate chronology may be discerned. The resulting mythological "history of the world" may be divided into three or four broader periods:
</p>
<ol><li><i>The myths of origin</i> or <i>age of gods (Theogonies, "births of gods")</i>: myths about the origins of the world, the gods, and the human race.</li>
<li><i>The age when gods and mortals mingled freely</i>: stories of the early interactions between gods, <a href="/wiki/Demigod" title="Demigod">demigods</a>, and mortals.</li>
<li><i> The age of heroes (heroic age)</i>, where divine activity was more limited. The last and greatest of the heroic legends is the story of <i>the Trojan War and after</i> (which is regarded by some researchers as a separate, fourth period).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles7_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Miles7-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 35">: 35 </span></sup></li></ol>
<p>While the age of gods often has been of more interest to contemporary students of myth, the Greek authors of the archaic and classical eras had a clear preference for the age of heroes, establishing a chronology and record of human accomplishments after the questions of how the world came into being were explained. For example, the heroic <i>Iliad</i> and <i>Odyssey</i> dwarfed the divine-focused <i>Theogony</i> and Homeric Hymns in both size and popularity. Under the influence of Homer the "hero cult" leads to a restructuring in spiritual life, expressed in the separation of the realm of the gods from the realm of the dead (heroes), of the <a href="/wiki/Chthonic" title="Chthonic">Chthonic</a> from the Olympian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 205">: 205 </span></sup> In the <i>Works and Days</i>, Hesiod makes use of a scheme of Four <a href="/wiki/Ages_of_Man" title="Ages of Man">Ages of Man</a> (or Races): Golden, Silver, Bronze, and Iron. These races or ages are separate creations of the gods, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a> belonging to the reign of Cronos, the subsequent races to the creation of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>. The presence of evil was explained by the myth of <a href="/wiki/Pandora" title="Pandora">Pandora</a>, when all of the best of human capabilities, save hope, had been spilled out of her overturned jar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worksanddays_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worksanddays-22">[21]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i>, Ovid follows Hesiod's concept of the four ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ovid89-162_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ovid89-162-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_the_world_and_the_gods">Origins of the world and the gods</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_primordial_gods" title="Greek primordial gods">Greek primordial gods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Family_tree_of_the_Greek_gods" title="Family tree of the Greek gods">Family tree of the Greek gods</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amor_Vincet_Omnia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1614" data-file-width="1151" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Amor_Vincet_Omnia.jpg/170px-Amor_Vincet_Omnia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Amor_Vincet_Omnia.jpg/255px-Amor_Vincet_Omnia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Amor_Vincet_Omnia.jpg/340px-Amor_Vincet_Omnia.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amor_Vincet_Omnia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Amor_Vincit_Omnia_(Caravaggio)" title="Amor Vincit Omnia (Caravaggio)">Amor Vincit Omnia</a></i> (<i>Love Conquers All</i>), a depiction of the god of love, Eros. By <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Michelangelo Merisi da Caravaggio</a>, circa 1601–1602.</div></div></div>
<p>"Myths of origin" or "<a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">creation myths</a>" represent an attempt to explain the beginnings of the universe in human language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klatt-Brazouskixii_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Klatt-Brazouskixii-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 10">: 10 </span></sup> The most widely accepted version at the time, although a philosophical account of the beginning of things, is reported by <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>. He begins with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaos_(mythology)" title="Chaos (mythology)">Chaos</a>, a yawning nothingness. Out of the void emerged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a> (the Earth) and some other primary divine beings: <a href="/wiki/Eros" title="Eros">Eros</a> (Love), the <a href="/wiki/Abyss_(religion)" title="Abyss (religion)">Abyss</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Tartarus" title="Tartarus">Tartarus</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Erebus" title="Erebus">Erebus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theogony116-138_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theogony116-138-24">[23]</a></sup> Without male assistance, Gaia gave birth to <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Uranus</a> (the Sky) who then fertilized her. From that union were born first the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a>—six males: <a href="/wiki/Coeus" title="Coeus">Coeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crius" title="Crius">Crius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperion_(mythology)" title="Hyperion (mythology)">Hyperion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iapetus_(mythology)" title="Iapetus (mythology)">Iapetus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a>; and six females: <a href="/wiki/Mnemosyne" title="Mnemosyne">Mnemosyne</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoebe_(mythology)" title="Phoebe (mythology)">Phoebe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(mythology)" title="Rhea (mythology)">Rhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theia" title="Theia">Theia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Themis" title="Themis">Themis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(mythology)" title="Tethys (mythology)">Tethys</a>. After Cronus was born, Gaia and Uranus decreed no more Titans were to be born. They were followed by the one-eyed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclopes</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hecatonchires" title="Hecatonchires">Hecatonchires</a> or Hundred-Handed Ones, who were both thrown into Tartarus by Uranus. This made Gaia furious. Cronus ("the wily, youngest and most terrible of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia's</a> children"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theogony116-138_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Theogony116-138-24">[23]</a></sup> was convinced by Gaia to castrate his father. He did this and became the ruler of the Titans with his sister-wife, Rhea, as his consort, and the other Titans became his court.
</p><p>A motif of father-against-son conflict was repeated when Cronus was confronted by his son, <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>. Because Cronus had betrayed his father, he feared that his offspring would do the same, and so each time Rhea gave birth, he snatched up the child and ate it. Rhea hated this and tricked him by hiding Zeus and wrapping a stone in a baby's blanket, which Cronus ate. When Zeus was full-grown, he fed Cronus a drugged drink which caused him to vomit, throwing up Rhea's other children, including <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hestia" title="Hestia">Hestia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>, and the stone, which had been sitting in Cronus's stomach all this time. Zeus then challenged Cronus to <a href="/wiki/Titanomachy" title="Titanomachy">war</a> for the kingship of the gods. At last, with the help of the Cyclopes (whom Zeus freed from Tartarus), Zeus and his siblings were victorious, while Cronus and the Titans were hurled down to imprisonment in <a href="/wiki/Tartarus" title="Tartarus">Tartarus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theogony713-735_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theogony713-735-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amphora_birth_Athena_Louvre_F32.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1675" data-file-width="1866" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Amphora_birth_Athena_Louvre_F32.jpg/220px-Amphora_birth_Athena_Louvre_F32.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Amphora_birth_Athena_Louvre_F32.jpg/330px-Amphora_birth_Athena_Louvre_F32.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Amphora_birth_Athena_Louvre_F32.jpg/440px-Amphora_birth_Athena_Louvre_F32.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amphora_birth_Athena_Louvre_F32.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Attic black-figured <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphora</a> depicting Athena being "reborn" from the head of Zeus, who had swallowed her mother <a href="/wiki/Metis_(mythology)" title="Metis (mythology)">Metis</a>, on the right, Eileithyia, the  goddess of childbirth, assists, circa 550–525 BC (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre" title="Musée du Louvre">Musée du Louvre</a>, Paris).</div></div></div>
<p>Zeus was plagued by the same concern, and after a prophecy that the offspring of his first wife, <a href="/wiki/Metis_(mythology)" title="Metis (mythology)">Metis</a>, would give birth to a god "greater than he", Zeus swallowed her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 98">: 98 </span></sup> She was already <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnant</a> with <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, however, and she burst forth from his head—fully-grown and dressed for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 108">: 108 </span></sup>
</p><p>The earliest Greek thought about poetry considered the theogonies to be the prototypical poetic genre—the prototypical <i>mythos</i>—and imputed almost magical powers to it. <a href="/wiki/Orpheus" title="Orpheus">Orpheus</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archetypal" title="Archetypal">archetypal</a> poet, also was the archetypal singer of theogonies, which he uses to calm seas and storms in Apollonius' <i><a href="/wiki/Argonautica" title="Argonautica">Argonautica</a></i>, and to move the stony hearts of the underworld gods in his descent to <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a>. When <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> invents the <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a> in the <i>Homeric Hymn to Hermes</i>, the first thing he does is sing about the birth of the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hermes_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hermes-27">[26]</a></sup> Hesiod's <i>Theogony</i> is not only the fullest surviving account of the gods but also the fullest surviving account of the archaic poet's function, with its long preliminary invocation to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muse" title="Muse">Muses</a>. Theogony also was the subject of many lost poems, including those attributed to Orpheus, <a href="/wiki/Musaeus_of_Athens" title="Musaeus of Athens">Musaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epimenides" title="Epimenides">Epimenides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abaris_the_Hyperborean" title="Abaris the Hyperborean">Abaris</a>, and other legendary seers, which were used in private ritual purifications and <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_mysteries" title="Greco-Roman mysteries">mystery-rites</a>. There are indications that <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> was familiar with some version of the Orphic theogony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Betegh147_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Betegh147-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 147">: 147 </span></sup> A silence would have been expected about religious rites and beliefs, however, and that nature of the culture would not have been reported by members of the society while the beliefs were held. After they ceased to become religious beliefs, few would have known the rites and rituals. Allusions often existed, however, to aspects that were quite public.
</p><p>Images existed on pottery and religious artwork that were interpreted and more likely, misinterpreted in many diverse myths and tales. A few fragments of these works survive in quotations by <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonist</a> philosophers and recently unearthed <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> scraps. One of these scraps, the <a href="/wiki/Derveni_papyrus" title="Derveni papyrus">Derveni Papyrus</a> now proves that at least in the fifth-century BC a theogonic-cosmogonic poem of Orpheus was in existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 236">: 236 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Betegh147_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Betegh147-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 147">: 147 </span></sup>
</p><p>The first philosophical cosmologists reacted against, or sometimes built upon, popular mythical conceptions that had existed in the Greek world for some time. Some of these popular conceptions can be gleaned from the poetry of Homer and Hesiod. In Homer, the Earth was viewed as a flat disk afloat on the river of <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a> and overlooked by a hemispherical sky with sun, moon, and stars. The Sun (<a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>) traversed the heavens as a charioteer and sailed around the Earth in a golden bowl at night. Sun, earth, heaven, rivers, and winds could be addressed in prayers and called to witness oaths. Natural fissures were popularly regarded as entrances to the subterranean house of Hades and his predecessors, home of the dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrAlga_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrAlga-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 45">: 45 </span></sup> Influences from other cultures always afforded new themes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_pantheon">Greek pantheon</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">Ancient Greek religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Olympians" title="Twelve Olympians">Twelve Olympians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_Tree_of_the_Greek_Gods" title="Family Tree of the Greek Gods">Family Tree of the Greek Gods</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Mycenaean_gods" title="List of Mycenaean gods">List of Mycenaean gods</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leda_-_after_Michelangelo_Buonarroti.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="677" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Leda_-_after_Michelangelo_Buonarroti.jpg/220px-Leda_-_after_Michelangelo_Buonarroti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Leda_-_after_Michelangelo_Buonarroti.jpg/330px-Leda_-_after_Michelangelo_Buonarroti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Leda_-_after_Michelangelo_Buonarroti.jpg/440px-Leda_-_after_Michelangelo_Buonarroti.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leda_-_after_Michelangelo_Buonarroti.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zeus, disguised as a <a href="/wiki/Swan" title="Swan">swan</a>, seduces <a href="/wiki/Leda_(mythology)" title="Leda (mythology)">Leda</a>, the Queen of <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>. A sixteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Leda_and_the_Swan_(Michelangelo)" title="Leda and the Swan (Michelangelo)">copy of the lost original by Michelangelo</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>According to Classical-era mythology, after the overthrow of the Titans, the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God_(male_deity)" title="God (male deity)">gods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Goddess" title="Goddess">goddesses</a> was confirmed. Among the principal Greek gods were the Olympians, residing on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Olympus" title="Mount Olympus">Mount Olympus</a> under the eye of Zeus. (The limitation of their number to twelve seems to have been a comparatively modern idea.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoll8_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stoll8-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8">: 8 </span></sup> Besides the Olympians, the Greeks worshipped various gods of the countryside, the satyr-god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan_(mythology)" title="Pan (mythology)">Pan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nymph" title="Nymph">Nymphs</a> (spirits of rivers), <a href="/wiki/Naiad" title="Naiad">Naiads</a> (who dwelled in springs), <a href="/wiki/Dryad" title="Dryad">Dryads</a> (who were spirits of the trees), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nereid" title="Nereid">Nereids</a> (who inhabited the sea), river gods, <a href="/wiki/Satyr" title="Satyr">Satyrs</a>, and others. In addition, there were the dark powers of the underworld, such as the <a href="/wiki/Erinyes" title="Erinyes">Erinyes</a> (or Furies), said to pursue those guilty of crimes against blood-relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrRel_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrRel-31">[30]</a></sup> In order to honor the Ancient Greek pantheon, poets composed the Homeric Hymns (a group of thirty-three songs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cashford174_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cashford174-32">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Nagy" title="Gregory Nagy">Gregory Nagy</a> (1992) regards "the larger Homeric Hymns as simple preludes (compared with <i>Theogony</i>), each of which invokes one god."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagy54_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nagy54-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 54">: 54 </span></sup>
</p><p>The gods of Greek mythology are described as having essentially corporeal but ideal bodies. According to <a href="/wiki/Walter_Burkert" title="Walter Burkert">Walter Burkert</a>, the defining characteristic of Greek anthropomorphism is that "the Greek gods are persons, not abstractions, ideas or concepts."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 182">: 182 </span></sup> Regardless of their underlying forms, the Ancient Greek gods have many fantastic abilities; most significantly, the gods are not affected by disease, and can be wounded only under highly unusual circumstances. The Greeks considered immortality as the distinctive characteristic of their gods; this immortality, as well as unfading youth, was insured by the constant use of <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ambrosia" title="Ambrosia">ambrosia</a>, by which the divine blood was renewed in their veins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoll8_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stoll8-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 4">: 4 </span></sup>
</p><p>Each god descends from his or her own genealogy, pursues differing interests, has a certain area of expertise, and is governed by a unique personality; however, these descriptions arise from a multiplicity of archaic local variants, which do not always agree with one another. When these gods are called upon in poetry, prayer, or cult, they are referred to by a combination of their name and <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithets</a>, that identify them by these distinctions from other manifestations of themselves (e.g., <i>Apollo Musagetes</i> is "<a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>, [as] leader of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muse" title="Muse">Muses</a>"). Alternatively, the epithet may identify a particular and localized aspect of the god, sometimes thought to be already ancient during the classical epoch of Greece.
</p><p>Most gods were associated with specific aspects of life. For example, <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a> was the goddess of love and beauty, <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a> was the god of war, <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a> the ruler of the underworld, and <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> the goddess of wisdom and courage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoll8_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stoll8-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 20ff">: 20ff </span></sup> Some gods, such as <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>, revealed complex personalities and mixtures of functions, while others, such as <a href="/wiki/Hestia" title="Hestia">Hestia</a> (literally "hearth") and <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a> (literally "sun"), were little more than personifications. The most impressive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_temple" title="Greek temple">temples</a> tended to be dedicated to a limited number of gods, who were the focus of large pan-Hellenic cults. It was, however, common for individual regions and villages to devote their own cults to minor gods. Many cities also honored the more well-known gods with unusual local rites and associated strange myths with them that were unknown elsewhere. During the heroic age, the cult of heroes (or demigods) supplemented that of the gods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_gods_and_mortals">Age of gods and mortals</span></h3>
<p>Bridging the age when gods lived alone and the age when divine interference in human affairs was limited was a transitional age in which gods and mortals moved together. These were the early days of the world when the groups mingled more freely than they did later. Most of these tales were later told by Ovid's <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i> and they are often divided into two thematic groups: tales of love, and tales of punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles7_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Miles7-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 38">: 38 </span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dionysos_satyrs_Cdm_Paris_575.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1940" data-file-width="1940" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Dionysos_satyrs_Cdm_Paris_575.jpg/220px-Dionysos_satyrs_Cdm_Paris_575.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Dionysos_satyrs_Cdm_Paris_575.jpg/330px-Dionysos_satyrs_Cdm_Paris_575.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Dionysos_satyrs_Cdm_Paris_575.jpg/440px-Dionysos_satyrs_Cdm_Paris_575.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dionysos_satyrs_Cdm_Paris_575.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a> with <a href="/wiki/Satyr" title="Satyr">satyrs</a>. Interior of a cup painted by the <a href="/wiki/Brygos_Painter" title="Brygos Painter">Brygos Painter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_des_M%C3%A9dailles" title="Cabinet des Médailles">Cabinet des Médailles</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Tales of love often involve incest, or the seduction or rape of a mortal woman by a male god, resulting in heroic offspring. The stories generally suggest that relationships between gods and mortals are something to avoid; even consenting relationships rarely have happy endings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miles7_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Miles7-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 39">: 39 </span></sup> In a few cases, a female divinity mates with a mortal man, as in the <i>Homeric Hymn to Aphrodite</i>, where the goddess lies with <a href="/wiki/Anchises" title="Anchises">Anchises</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The second type (tales of punishment) involves the appropriation or invention of some important cultural artifact, as when <a href="/wiki/Prometheus" title="Prometheus">Prometheus</a> steals fire from the gods, when <a href="/wiki/Tantalus" title="Tantalus">Tantalus</a> steals nectar and <a href="/wiki/Ambrosia" title="Ambrosia">ambrosia</a> from Zeus' table and gives it to his subjects—revealing to them the secrets of the gods, when <a href="/wiki/Prometheus" title="Prometheus">Prometheus</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lycaon_(Greek_myth)" title="Lycaon (Greek myth)">Lycaon</a> invents sacrifice, when <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> teaches agriculture and the <a href="/wiki/Eleusinian_Mysteries" title="Eleusinian Mysteries">Mysteries</a> to <a href="/wiki/Triptolemus" title="Triptolemus">Triptolemus</a>, or when <a href="/wiki/Marsyas" title="Marsyas">Marsyas</a> invents the <a href="/wiki/Aulos" title="Aulos">aulos</a> and enters into a musical contest with <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>. Ian Morris considers Prometheus' adventures as "a place between the history of the gods and that of man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morris291_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morris291-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 291">: 291 </span></sup> An anonymous papyrus fragment, dated to the third century, vividly portrays <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>' punishment of the king of <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycurgus_(Thrace)" title="Lycurgus (Thrace)">Lycurgus</a>, whose recognition of the new god came too late, resulting in horrific penalties that extended into the afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver335_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weaver335-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 50">: 50 </span></sup> The story of the arrival of Dionysus to establish his cult in Thrace was also the subject of an Aeschylean trilogy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bushnell28_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bushnell28-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 28">: 28 </span></sup> In another tragedy, Euripides' <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bacchae" title="The Bacchae">The Bacchae</a></i>, the king of <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentheus" title="Pentheus">Pentheus</a>, is punished by Dionysus, because he disrespected the god and spied on his <a href="/wiki/Maenad" title="Maenad">Maenads</a>, the female <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worshippers</a> of the god.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trobe195_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trobe195-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 195">: 195 </span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eleusinian_hydria_Antikensammlung_Berlin_1984.46_n2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1174" data-file-width="1370" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Eleusinian_hydria_Antikensammlung_Berlin_1984.46_n2.jpg/220px-Eleusinian_hydria_Antikensammlung_Berlin_1984.46_n2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Eleusinian_hydria_Antikensammlung_Berlin_1984.46_n2.jpg/330px-Eleusinian_hydria_Antikensammlung_Berlin_1984.46_n2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Eleusinian_hydria_Antikensammlung_Berlin_1984.46_n2.jpg/440px-Eleusinian_hydria_Antikensammlung_Berlin_1984.46_n2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eleusinian_hydria_Antikensammlung_Berlin_1984.46_n2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metanira" title="Metanira">Metanira</a> in a detail on an Apulian red-figure hydria, circa 340 BC (<a href="/wiki/Altes_Museum" title="Altes Museum">Altes Museum</a>, Berlin).</div></div></div>
<p>In another story, based on an old folktale-motif,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nilsson50_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nilsson50-39">[38]</a></sup> and echoing a similar theme, <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a> was searching for her daughter, <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a>, having taken the form of an old woman called Doso, and received a hospitable welcome from <a href="/wiki/Celeus" title="Celeus">Celeus</a>, the King of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eleusis" title="Eleusis">Eleusis</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attica,_Greece" title="Attica, Greece">Attica</a>. As a gift to Celeus, because of his hospitality, Demeter planned to make his son <a href="/wiki/Demophon_of_Eleusis" title="Demophon of Eleusis">Demophon</a> a god, but she was unable to complete the ritual because his mother <a href="/wiki/Metanira" title="Metanira">Metanira</a> walked in and saw her son in the fire and screamed in fright, which angered Demeter, who lamented that foolish mortals do not understand the concept and ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demeter_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demeter-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heroic_age">Heroic age</span></h3>
<p>The age in which the heroes lived is known as the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Heroic_Age" title="Greek Heroic Age">Heroic age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelsey30_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelsey30-41">[40]</a></sup> The epic and genealogical poetry created cycles of stories clustered around particular heroes or events and established the family relationships between the heroes of different stories; they thus arranged the stories in sequence. According to <a href="/wiki/Ken_Dowden" title="Ken Dowden">Ken Dowden</a> (1992), "there is even a saga effect: We can follow the fates of some families in successive generations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowden11_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dowden11-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 11">: 11 </span></sup>
</p><p>After the rise of the hero cult, gods and heroes constitute the sacral sphere and are invoked together in oaths and prayers which are addressed to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 205">: 205 </span></sup> Burkert (2002) notes that "the roster of heroes, again in contrast to the gods, is never given fixed and final form. Great gods are no longer born, but new heroes can always be raised up from the army of the dead." Another important difference between the hero cult and the cult of gods is that the hero becomes the centre of local group identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 206">: 206 </span></sup>
</p><p>The monumental events of Heracles are regarded as the dawn of the age of heroes. To the Heroic Age are also ascribed three great events: the <a href="/wiki/Argonauts" title="Argonauts">Argonautic</a> expedition, the <a href="/wiki/Theban_Cycle" title="Theban Cycle">Theban Cycle</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelsey30_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kelsey30-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 340">: 340 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heracles_and_the_Heracleidae">Heracles and the Heracleidae</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heracleidae" title="Heracleidae">Heracleidae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hercules" title="Hercules">Hercules</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herakles_and_Telephos_Louvre_MR219.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3640" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Herakles_and_Telephos_Louvre_MR219.jpg/170px-Herakles_and_Telephos_Louvre_MR219.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Herakles_and_Telephos_Louvre_MR219.jpg/255px-Herakles_and_Telephos_Louvre_MR219.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Herakles_and_Telephos_Louvre_MR219.jpg/340px-Herakles_and_Telephos_Louvre_MR219.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herakles_and_Telephos_Louvre_MR219.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a> with his baby <a href="/wiki/Telephus" title="Telephus">Telephus</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>, Paris).</div></div></div>
<p>Some scholars believe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 10">: 10 </span></sup> that behind Heracles' complicated mythology there was probably a real man, perhaps a chieftain-vassal of the kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Argos" title="Ancient Argos">Argos</a>. Some scholars suggest the story of Heracles is an allegory for the sun's yearly passage through the twelve constellations of the zodiac.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dupuis_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dupuis-43">[42]</a></sup> Others point to earlier myths from other cultures, showing the story of Heracles as a local adaptation of hero myths already well established. Traditionally, Heracles was the son of Zeus and <a href="/wiki/Alcmene" title="Alcmene">Alcmene</a>, granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/Perseus" title="Perseus">Perseus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrHer_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrHer-44">[43]</a></sup> His fantastic solitary exploits, with their many <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folk-tale</a> themes, provided much material for popular legend. According to Burkert (2002), "He is portrayed as a sacrificer, mentioned as a founder of altars, and imagined as a voracious eater himself; it is in this role that he appears in comedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>While his tragic end provided much material for tragedy—<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heracles_(Euripides)" title="Heracles (Euripides)">Heracles</a></i> is regarded by Thalia Papadopoulou as "a play of great significance in examination of other Euripidean dramas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PapadopoulouBurkert_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-PapadopoulouBurkert-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 211">: 211 </span></sup> In art and literature Heracles was represented as an enormously strong man of moderate height; his characteristic weapon was the bow but frequently also the club. Vase paintings demonstrate the unparalleled popularity of Heracles, his fight with the lion being depicted many hundreds of times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-7"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 211">: 211 </span></sup>
</p><p>Heracles also entered Etruscan and Roman mythology and cult, and the exclamation "mehercule" became as familiar to the Romans<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> as "Herakleis" was to the Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-8"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 211">: 211 </span></sup> In Italy he was worshipped as a god of merchants and traders, although others also prayed to him for his characteristic gifts of good luck or rescue from danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrHer_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrHer-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Heracles attained the highest social prestige through his appointment as official ancestor of the <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">Dorian</a> kings. This probably served as a legitimation for the Dorian migrations into the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hyllus" title="Hyllus">Hyllus</a>, the eponymous hero of one Dorian <a href="/wiki/Phyle" title="Phyle">phyle</a>, became the son of Heracles and one of the <i>Heracleidae</i> or <i>Heraclids</i> (the numerous descendants of Heracles, especially the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Hyllus" title="Hyllus">Hyllus</a>—other Heracleidae included <a href="/wiki/Macaria" title="Macaria">Macaria</a>, Lamos, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manto_(Greek_Mythology)" title="Manto (Greek Mythology)">Manto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bianor" title="Bianor">Bianor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tlepolemus" title="Tlepolemus">Tlepolemus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Telephus" title="Telephus">Telephus</a>). These Heraclids conquered the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnesian</a> kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Argos" title="Ancient Argos">Argos</a>, claiming, according to legend, a right to rule them through their ancestor. Their rise to dominance is frequently called the "<a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a>". The Lydian and later the Macedonian kings, as rulers of the same rank, also became Heracleidae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BurkertHer_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-BurkertHer-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffan-Barket205_21-9"><a href="#cite_note-Raffan-Barket205-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 211">: 211 </span></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bellerophon_riding_Pegasus_and_killing_the_Chimera,_Roman_mosaic,_the_Rolin_Museum_in_Autun,_France,_2nd_to_3rd_century_AD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1906" data-file-width="1866" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Bellerophon_riding_Pegasus_and_killing_the_Chimera%2C_Roman_mosaic%2C_the_Rolin_Museum_in_Autun%2C_France%2C_2nd_to_3rd_century_AD.jpg/170px-Bellerophon_riding_Pegasus_and_killing_the_Chimera%2C_Roman_mosaic%2C_the_Rolin_Museum_in_Autun%2C_France%2C_2nd_to_3rd_century_AD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Bellerophon_riding_Pegasus_and_killing_the_Chimera%2C_Roman_mosaic%2C_the_Rolin_Museum_in_Autun%2C_France%2C_2nd_to_3rd_century_AD.jpg/255px-Bellerophon_riding_Pegasus_and_killing_the_Chimera%2C_Roman_mosaic%2C_the_Rolin_Museum_in_Autun%2C_France%2C_2nd_to_3rd_century_AD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Bellerophon_riding_Pegasus_and_killing_the_Chimera%2C_Roman_mosaic%2C_the_Rolin_Museum_in_Autun%2C_France%2C_2nd_to_3rd_century_AD.jpg/340px-Bellerophon_riding_Pegasus_and_killing_the_Chimera%2C_Roman_mosaic%2C_the_Rolin_Museum_in_Autun%2C_France%2C_2nd_to_3rd_century_AD.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bellerophon_riding_Pegasus_and_killing_the_Chimera,_Roman_mosaic,_the_Rolin_Museum_in_Autun,_France,_2nd_to_3rd_century_AD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bellerophon" title="Bellerophon">Bellerophon</a> riding <a href="/wiki/Pegasus" title="Pegasus">Pegasus</a> and slaying the <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(mythology)" title="Chimera (mythology)">Chimera</a>, central medallion of a <a href="/wiki/Roman_mosaic" title="Roman mosaic">Roman mosaic</a> from <a href="/wiki/Autun" title="Autun">Autun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Rolin" title="Musée Rolin">Musée Rolin</a>, 2nd to 3rd century AD</div></div></div>
<p>Other members of this earliest generation of heroes such as Perseus, <a href="/wiki/Deucalion" title="Deucalion">Deucalion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theseus" title="Theseus">Theseus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bellerophon" title="Bellerophon">Bellerophon</a>, have many traits in common with Heracles. Like him, their exploits are solitary, fantastic and border on <a href="/wiki/Fairy_tale" title="Fairy tale">fairy tale</a>, as they slay monsters such as the <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(mythology)" title="Chimera (mythology)">Chimera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medusa" title="Medusa">Medusa</a>. Bellerophon's adventures are commonplace types, similar to the adventures of Heracles and Theseus. Sending a hero to his presumed death is also a recurrent theme of this early heroic tradition, used in the cases of Perseus and Bellerophon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Argonauts">Argonauts</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Argonauts" title="Argonauts">Argonauts</a></div>
<p>The only surviving Hellenistic epic, the <i><a href="/wiki/Argonautica" title="Argonautica">Argonautica</a></i> of Apollonius of Rhodes (epic poet, scholar, and director of the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a>) tells the myth of the voyage of <a href="/wiki/Jason" title="Jason">Jason</a> and the Argonauts to retrieve the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Fleece" title="Golden Fleece">Golden Fleece</a> from the mythical land of <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchis</a>. In the <i>Argonautica</i>, Jason is impelled on his quest by king <a href="/wiki/Pelias" title="Pelias">Pelias</a>, who receives a prophecy that a man with one sandal would be his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nemesis_(mythology)" title="Nemesis (mythology)">nemesis</a>. Jason loses a sandal in a river, arrives at the court of Pelias, and the epic is set in motion. Nearly every member of the next generation of heroes, as well as Heracles, went with Jason in the ship <i><a href="/wiki/Argo" title="Argo">Argo</a></i> to fetch the Golden Fleece. This generation also included <a href="/wiki/Theseus" title="Theseus">Theseus</a>, who went to <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> to slay the <a href="/wiki/Minotaur" title="Minotaur">Minotaur</a>; <a href="/wiki/Atalanta" title="Atalanta">Atalanta</a>, the female heroine, and <a href="/wiki/Meleager" title="Meleager">Meleager</a>, who once had an epic cycle of his own to rival the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Pindar" title="Pindar">Pindar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Rhodes" title="Apollonius of Rhodes">Apollonius</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_(Pseudo-Apollodorus)" title="Bibliotheca (Pseudo-Apollodorus)">Bibliotheca</a></i> endeavor to give full lists of the Argonauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApApPin_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApApPin-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Apollonius wrote his poem in the 3rd century BC, the composition of the story of the Argonauts is earlier than <i>Odyssey</i>, which shows familiarity with the exploits of Jason (the wandering of Odysseus may have been partly founded on it).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrArgGr_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrArgGr-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-52">[51]</a></sup> In ancient times the expedition was regarded as a historical fact, an incident in the opening up of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> to Greek commerce and colonization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrArgGr_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrArgGr-51">[50]</a></sup> It was also extremely popular, forming a cycle to which a number of local legends became attached. The story of <a href="/wiki/Medea" title="Medea">Medea</a>, in particular, caught the imagination of the tragic poets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Atreus_and_Theban_Cycle">House of Atreus and Theban Cycle</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Theban_Cycle" title="Theban Cycle">Theban Cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seven_against_Thebes" title="Seven against Thebes">Seven against Thebes</a></div>
<p>In between the Argo and the Trojan War, there was a generation known chiefly for its horrific crimes. This includes the doings of <a href="/wiki/Atreus" title="Atreus">Atreus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thyestes" title="Thyestes">Thyestes</a> at Argos. Behind the myth of the house of Atreus (one of the two principal heroic dynasties with the house of <a href="/wiki/Labdacus" title="Labdacus">Labdacus</a>) lies the problem of the devolution of power and of the mode of accession to sovereignty. The twins Atreus and Thyestes with their descendants played the leading role in the tragedy of the devolution of power in Mycenae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonnefoy103_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonnefoy103-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Theban Cycle deals with events associated especially with <a href="/wiki/Cadmus" title="Cadmus">Cadmus</a>, the city's founder, and later with the doings of <a href="/wiki/Laius" title="Laius">Laius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oedipus" title="Oedipus">Oedipus</a> at Thebes; a series of stories that lead to the war of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_against_Thebes" title="Seven against Thebes">Seven against Thebes</a> and the eventual pillage of that city at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Epigoni" title="Epigoni">Epigoni</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hard1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hard1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 317">: 317 </span></sup> (It is not known whether the Seven figured in early epic.) As far as Oedipus is concerned, early epic accounts seem to have him continuing to rule at Thebes after the revelation that <a href="/wiki/Jocasta" title="Jocasta">Iokaste</a> was his mother, and subsequently marrying a second wife who becomes the mother of his children—markedly different from the tale known to us through tragedy (e.g. Sophocles' <i><a href="/wiki/Oedipus_Rex" title="Oedipus Rex">Oedipus Rex</a></i>) and later mythological accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hard1_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hard1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 311">: 311 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trojan_War_and_aftermath">Trojan War and aftermath</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Trojan_War" title="Trojan War">Trojan War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epic_Cycle" title="Epic Cycle">Epic Cycle</a></div>
